Chris Barker
http://ling.ucsd.edu/~barker


             How many syllables does English have?


There are several ways this question could be interpreted.  The
interpretation I have in mind is equivalent to asking the following
question:

   How many symbols would it take to provide a syllabary for English?

This file contains below an approximate list of all syllable types
that occur in some English word.  Because the main question concerns
writing systems for spoken English, we're concerned here with
pronunciation, not spelling; thus "two" and "too" are two tokens of
the same syllable type, since they sound the same.

One of the interesting results of this study is the degree to which
English fails to take advantage of the full range of syllables it
might have used, even for some quite simple syllable structures.  For
instance, in CVC syllables (such as "tip" or "lam"), there are very
few restrictions on the consonants in English.  [There are a few
restrictions, however, such as syllables may not start with "ng" and
may not end with "h".]  Since the transcription below uses 24
consonants and 20 vowels, we would expect 24 * 20 * 24 = 11,520 CVC
syllables if English used them all.  But there are only 2,756 distinct
CVC syllables in the BEEP dictionary (that my imperfect
syllabification algorithm found), roughly a forth of the full possible
range.  For instance, we do have these:

f uw d           SEAFOOD
f uw k           FUCHSINE
f uw l           TOMFOOL
f uw n           TYPHOON

But there are no English words that contain the following syllables,
even though they are perfectly well-formed according to the
phonotactics of English:

f uw p
f uw b
f uw t
f uw f
f uw v
f uw th
f uw dh
f uw s
f uw z
f uw sh
f uw zh
f uw ch
f uw jh
f uw k
f uw ng
f uw g
f uw m
f uw r

[Note that in some cases this is a flaw in the coverage of the
dictionary, since many speakers of English have, e.g., "foos-ball" as
a word.]

My data comes from the BEEP pronouncing dictionary, a descendent of
the CUVOLAD dictionary.  See

  http://svr-www.eng.cam.ac.uk/comp.speech/Section1/Lexical/beep.html

for a downloadable version of the dictionary.  I used version 6.0,
which contained pronunciations of just over 16,000 words.  (Note that
"top" and "tops" counted as distinct words, since they are pronounced
differently.)  It is important to remember that this dictionary
contains phonemic transcriptions of British pronunciation, so that the
word "arched", for instance, is transcribed as "aa ch t", i.e.,
without the "r" that is present in the American pronunciation.

Here is a complete list of the phoneme transcriptions used by the BEEP
dictionary (20 vowels, 24 consonants):

aa     odd
ae     at
ah     hut
ao     ought
aw     cow
ax     abaft  (first vowel; schwa)
ay     hide
ea     wear   (remember, this is Britsh pronunciation)
eh     Ed
er     hurt
ey     ate
ia     fortieth  (second vowel)
ih     it
iy     teen
oh     mob
ow     lobe
oy     toy
ua     intellectual  (last vowel)
uh     nook
uw     two

p      pick
b      be
t      tip
d      dee
f      fee
v      vise
th     thick
dh     thee   (eth)
s      sick
z      zip
sh     ship
zh     seizure
ch     cheese
jh     jeep
k      key
ng     rang   (engma)
g      green
m      me
n      new
l      lee
r      ream
w      win
y      you
hh     he

The most complex syllables had 7 phonemes, including

g l ih m p s t   GLIMPSED
p r oh m p t s   PROMPTS
s k ah l p t s   SCULPTS
s k r ah n ch t  SCRUNCHED
s k r aw n jh d  SCROUNGED
s k r ih m p s   SCRIMPS
s k r ih p t s   SCRIPTS
s k w eh l ch t  SQUELCHED
s k w ih n t s   SQUINTS
s p l aa n t s   TRANSPLANTS'
s p l ih n t s   SPLINTS
s p r ih n jh d  SPRINGED
s p r ih n t s   SPRINTS
s t ih ng k t s  INSTINCTS
s t r ae k t s   EXTRACTS
s t r ah k t s   REINSTRUCTS
s t r ah ng k s  STRUNK'S
s t r ah s t s   MISTRUSTS
s t r ax n t s   REGISTRANTS
s t r eh ng th s STRENGTHS
s t r eh ng th t STRENGTHED
s t r ey n jh d  ESTRANGED
s t r ey n t s   RESTRAINTS
s t r ih k t s   RESTRICTS
s t r ih n jh d  ASTRINGED
s t r oh n t s   RESTAURANTS'
t w eh l f th s  TWELFTHS

My syllabification algorithm pays attention to sonority, and is fairly
aggressive about syllabifying consonants as onsets whenever possible.

The syllabification algorithm is not particularly sophisticated or
elaborate.  As a result, this list contains a number of strings of
sounds that are not legitimate English syllables.  This is due to the
fact that my data do not recognize morpheme boundaries, even though
this affects syllabification.  For instance, the following two
putative syllables are not actually legitimate syllables of English:

   jh r ae k s     LUGGAGE-RACKS
   t w er k t      OUTWORKED

although they probably could have been.  If you're interested in
writing a better syllabification algorithm, my code is at

   http://ling.ucsd.edu/~barker/Syllables/syllabify.pl

and the full set of BEEP words syllabified by my algorithm is at

   http://ling.ucsd.edu/~barker/Syllables/words.syllabified.txt

There is a good chance that most, though probably not quite all,
legitimate syllables are represented below.

The grand total: 15831 syllable candidates.  Far too many to make a
syllabary worth the trouble.
